**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

**Target Repositories (version-pinned mirrors):**

- `github.com/sg-evals/terraform--7637a921` — use `repo:^github.com/sg-evals/terraform--7637a921$` filter

Scope ALL keyword_search/nls_search queries to these repos.
Use the repo name as the `repo` parameter for read_file/go_to_definition/find_references.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Team Handoff: Terraform State Backend Subsystem

## Scenario

You are taking over ownership of Terraform's state backend subsystem from a departing team member. The state backend is responsible for storing and managing Terraform state files, which contain the mapping between configured resources and real-world infrastructure.

Your team member has left limited documentation. Your task is to explore the codebase and produce a comprehensive handoff document that will help you and future team members understand, maintain, and extend the state backend system.

## Your Task

Explore the hashicorp/terraform codebase and create a structured handoff document covering the state backend subsystem. Your document should address the following sections:

### 1. Purpose
- What problem does the state backend subsystem solve?
- Why do we need different backend types (local, S3, remote, etc.)?
- What are the key responsibilities of a backend?

### 2. Dependencies
- What other Terraform subsystems does the backend interact with?
- What are the upstream dependencies (what calls into backends)?
- What are the downstream dependencies (what do backends call)?
- How does the backend system integrate with the broader Terraform architecture?

### 3. Relevant Components
- What are the main source files and directories for the backend subsystem?
- What modules/interfaces define the Backend interface?
- Where are concrete backend implementations located?
- What modules/interfaces define the state locking mechanism?
- What files are critical for understanding how backends work?

### 4. Failure Modes
- What can go wrong with state backends?
- How does the system handle state locking failures (stale locks, timeouts)?
- What happens when storage is unavailable?
- How are state corruption scenarios handled?
- What are common configuration errors and how are they detected?

### 5. Testing
- How are backends tested?
- What test patterns are used for backend implementations?
- Where are the backend tests located?
- How do you test state locking behavior?
- What integration tests exist for backends?

### 6. Debugging
- How do you troubleshoot state lock issues?
- How do you verify state consistency?
- What logs or diagnostics are available for debugging backend problems?
- How do you investigate stale locks or lock contention?

### 7. Adding a New Backend
- If you needed to add a new backend type (e.g., for a new cloud provider), what would be the step-by-step process?
- What interfaces need to be implemented?
- What files need to be created or modified?
- How is a new backend registered with the system?

## Deliverable

Create your handoff document as a markdown file at `/logs/agent/onboarding.md`.

Deliver a clear, well-structured document that covers all requested sections. Include:
- Specific file paths and directory names
- Key function/type names
- Code flow descriptions
- Concrete examples where helpful

## Evaluation

Your handoff document will be evaluated on:
- **Completeness**: All 7 sections addressed with substantive content
- **Accuracy**: Correct identification of relevant components, interfaces, and architectural patterns
- **Specificity**: Concrete file paths, type names, and code references (not generic descriptions)
- **Understanding**: Demonstrates comprehension of how the subsystem works, not just surface-level file listing

Good luck with your exploration!

## Document Overview

The handoff document at `/logs/agent/onboarding.md` covers all 7 requested sections:

### **1. Purpose** 
Explains the core problem the backend system solves (shared state management, persistence, flexibility) and why different backend types exist (local for dev, S3 for teams, Cloud for enterprise).

### **2. Dependencies**
Maps all relationships:
- **Upstream**: CLI command layer calling into backends
- **Internal**: State manager interfaces, configuration system, state objects
- **Downstream**: Storage providers (S3, GCS, Azure, etc.)
- **Architecture integration**: Shows how backends fit in the broader Terraform flow

### **3. Relevant Components**
Provides specific file locations and descriptions for:
- Core Backend interface (`internal/backend/backend.go`)
- State manager hierarchy (`internal/states/statemgr/`)
- Locking mechanism (`locker.go`)
- Backend registry (`internal/backend/init/init.go`)
- Local backend implementation
- 9 concrete remote state backends (S3, GCS, Azure, Consul, PostgreSQL, Kubernetes, HTTP, OSS, COS)
- Cloud/HCP Terraform backend

### **4. Failure Modes**
Details how the system handles:
- Configuration validation errors
- State locking failures and stale locks (with retry/backoff logic)
- Storage unavailability
- State consistency issues (serial/lineage mismatches)
- Concurrent modification prevention
- Workspace-specific errors

### **5. Testing**
Describes:
- Core test helpers (`TestBackendConfig`, `TestBackendStates`, `TestBackendStateLocks`)
- Test structure for new backends with concrete S3 example
- Acceptance test setup and gating
- Fake implementations for error testing

Callers must assume that\\n65: \\t// ephemeral portions of the state may be unpopulated after calling\\n66: \\t// RefreshState.\\n67: \\tRefreshState() error\\n68: }\\n69: \\n70: // Persister is the interface for managers that can write snapshots to\\n71: // persistent storage.\\n72: //\\n73: // Persister is usually implemented in conjunction with Writer, with\\n74: // PersistState copying the latest transient snapshot to be the new latest\\n75: // persistent snapshot.\\n76: //\\n77: // A Persister implementation must detect updates made by other processes\\n78: // that may be running concurrently and avoid destroying those changes. This method is primarily available\\n100: \\t// for testing and logging purposes, and is of little use otherwise.\\n101: \\tStateSnapshotMeta() SnapshotMeta\\n102: }\\n103: \\n104: // SnapshotMeta contains metadata about a persisted state snapshot.\\n105: //\\n106: // This metadata is usually (but not necessarily) included as part of the\\n107: // \\\"header\\\" of a state file, which is then written to a raw blob storage medium\\n108: // by a persistent state manager.\\n109: //\\n110: // Not all state managers will have useful values for all fields in this\\n111: // struct, so SnapshotMeta values are of little use beyond testing and logging\\n112: // use-cases.\\n113: type SnapshotMeta struct {\\n114: \\t// Lineage and Serial can be used to understand the relationships between\\n115: \\t// snapshots.\\n116: \\t//\\n117: \\t// If two snapshots both have an identical, non-empty Lineage\\n118: \\t// then the one with the higher Serial is newer than the other.\\n119: \\t// If the Lineage values are different or empty then the two snapshots\\n120: \\t// are unrelated and cannot be compared for relative age.\\n121: \\tLineage string\\n122: \\tSerial  uint64\\n123: \\n124: \\t// TerraformVersion is the number of the version of Terraform that created\\n125: \\t// the snapshot.\\n126: \\tTerraformVersion *version.Version\\n127: }\\n128: \\n129: // IntermediateStateConditionalPersister is an optional extension of\\n130: // [Persister] that allows an implementation to tailor the rules for\\n131: // whether to create intermediate state snapshots when Terraform Core emits\\n132: // events reporting that the state might have changed. This interface is used\\n133: // by the local backend when it's been configured to use another backend for\\n134: // state storage.\\n135: //\\n136: // For state managers that don't implement this interface, the local backend's\\n137: // StateHook uses a default set of rules that aim to be a good compromise\\n138: // between how long a state change can be active before it gets committed as a\\n139: // snapshot vs. how many intermediate snapshots will get created. That\\n140: // compromise is subject to change over time, but a state manager can implement\\n141: // this interface to exert full control over those rules.\\n142: type IntermediateStateConditionalPersister interface {\\n143: \\t// ShouldPersistIntermediateState will be called each time Terraform Core\\n144: \\t// emits an intermediate state event that is potentially eligible to be\\n145: \\t// persisted.\\n146: \\t//\\n147: \\t// The implemention should return true to signal that the state snapshot\\n148: \\t// most recently provided to the object's WriteState should be persisted,\\n149: \\t// or false if it should not be persisted.   "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # Terraform Core Architecture Summary\\n2: \\n3: This document is a summary of the main components of Terraform Core and how\\n4: data and requests flow between these components. It's intended as a primer\\n5: to help navigate the codebase to dig into more details.\\n6: \\n7: We assume some familiarity with user-facing Terraform concepts like\\n8: configuration, state, CLI workflow, etc. The Terraform website has\\n9: documentation on these ideas.\\n10: \\n11: ## Terraform Request Flow\\n12: \\n13: The following diagram shows an approximation of how a user command is\\n14: executed in Terraform:\\n15: \\n16: ![Terraform Architecture Diagram, described in text below](./images/architecture-overview.png)\\n17: \\n18: Each of the different subsystems (solid boxes) in this diagram is described\\n19: in more detail in a corresponding section below.\\n20: \\n21: ## CLI (`command` package)\\n22: \\n23: Each time a user runs the `terraform` program, aside from some initial\\n24: bootstrapping in the root package (not shown in the diagram) execution\\n25: transfers immediately into one of the \\\"command\\\" implementations in\\n26: [the `command` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/command).\\n27: The mapping between the user-facing command names and\\n28: their corresponding `command` package types can be found in the `commands.go`\\n29: file in the root of the repository.\\n30: \\n31: The full flow illustrated above does not actually apply to _all_ commands,\\n32: but it applies to the main Terraform workflow commands `terraform plan` and\\n33: `terraform apply`, along with a few others.\\n34: \\n35: For these commands, the role of the command implementation is to read and parse\\n36: any command line arguments, command line options, and environment variables\\n37: that are needed for the given command and use them to produce a\\n38: [`backend.Operation`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Operation)\\n39: object that describes an action to be taken.\\n40: \\n41: An _operation_ consists of:\\n42: \\n43: * The action to be taken (e.g. \\\"plan\\\", \\\"apply\\\").\\n44: * The name of the [workspace](https://www.terraform.io/docs/state/workspaces.html)\\n45:   where the action will be taken.\\n46: * Root module input variables to use for the action.\\n47: * For the \\\"plan\\\" operation, a path to the directory containing the configuration's root module.\\n48: * For the \\\"apply\\\" operation, the plan to apply.\\n49: * Various other less-common options/settings such as `-target` addresses, the\\n50: \\\"force\\\" flag, etc.\\n51: \\n52: The operation is then passed to the currently-selected\\n53: [backend](https://www.terraform.io/docs/backends/index.html). Each backend name\\n54: corresponds to an implementation of\\n55: [`backend.Backend`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Backend), using a\\n56: mapping table in\\n57: [the `backend/init` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/init).\\n58: \\n59: Backends that are able to execute operations additionally implement\\n60: [`backend.Enhanced`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Enhanced);\\n61: the command-handling code calls `Operation` with the operation it has\\n62: constructed, and then the backend is responsible for executing that action.\\n63: \\n64: Backends that execute operations, however, do so as an architectural implementation detail and not a\\n65: general feature of backends. That is, the term 'backend' as a Terraform feature is used to refer to\\n66: a plugin that determines where Terraform stores its state snapshots - only the default `local`\\n67: backend and HCP Terraform's backends (`remote`, `cloud`) perform operations.\\n68: \\n69: Thus, most backends do _not_ implement this interface, and so the `command` package wraps these\\n70: backends in an instance of\\n71: [`local.Local`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/local#Local),\\n72: causing the operation to be executed locally within the `terraform` process itself.\\n73: \\n74: ## Backends\\n75: \\n76: A _backend_ determines where Terraform should store its state snapshots.\\n77: \\n78: As described above, the `local` backend also executes operations on behalf of most other\\n79: backends. It uses a _state manager_\\n80: (either\\n81: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) if the\\n82: local backend is being used directly, or an implementation provided by whatever\\n83: backend is being wrapped) to retrieve the current state for the workspace\\n84: specified in the operation, then uses the _config loader_ to load and do\\n85: initial processing/validation of the configuration specified in the\\n86: operation. It then uses these, along with the other settings given in the\\n87: operation, to construct a\\n88: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context),\\n89: which is the main object that actually performs Terraform operations.\\n90: \\n91: The `local` backend finally calls an appropriate method on that context to\\n92: begin execution of the relevant command, such as\\n93: [`Plan`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context.Plan)\\n94: or\\n95: [`Apply`](), which in turn constructs a graph using a _graph builder_,\\n96: described in a later section.\\n97: \\n98: ## Configuration Loader\\n99: \\n100: The top-level configuration structure is represented by model types in\\n101: [package `configs`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs).\\n102: A whole configuration (the root module plus all of its descendent modules)\\n103: is represented by\\n104: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config).\\n105: \\n106: The `configs` package contains some low-level functionality for constructing\\n107: configuration objects, but the main entry point is in the sub-package\\n108: [`configload`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload]),\\n109: via\\n110: [`configload.Loader`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload#Loader).\\n111: A loader deals with all of the details of installing child modules\\n112: (during `terraform init`) and then locating those modules again when a\\n113: configuration is loaded by a backend. It takes the path to a root module\\n114: and recursively loads all of the child modules to produce a single\\n115: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config)\\n116: representing the entire configuration.\\n117: \\n118: Terraform expects configuration files written in the Terraform language, which\\n119: is a DSL built on top of\\n120: [HCL](https://github.com/hashicorp/hcl). Some parts of the configuration\\n121: cannot be interpreted until we build and walk the graph, since they depend\\n122: on the outcome of other parts of the configuration, and so these parts of\\n123: the configuration remain represented as the low-level HCL types\\n124: [`hcl.Body`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Body)\\n125: and\\n126: [`hcl.Expression`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Expression),\\n127: allowing Terraform to interpret them at a more appropriate time.\\n128: \\n129: ## State Manager\\n130: \\n131: A _state manager_ is responsible for storing and retrieving snapshots of the\\n132: [Terraform state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/index.html)\\n133: for a particular workspace. Each manager is an implementation of\\n134: some combination of interfaces in\\n135: [the `statemgr` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr),\\n136: with most practical managers implementing the full set of operations\\n137: described by\\n138: [`statemgr.Full`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Full)\\n139: provided by a _backend_. The smaller interfaces exist primarily for use in\\n140: other function signatures to be explicit about what actions the function might\\n141: take on the state manager; there is little reason to write a state manager\\n142: that does not implement all of `statemgr.Full`.\\n143: \\n144: The implementation\\n145: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) is used\\n146: by default (by the `local` backend) and is responsible for the familiar\\n147: `terraform.tfstate` local file that most Terraform users start with, before\\n148: they switch to [remote state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/remote.html).\\n149: Other implementations of `statemgr.Full` are used to implement remote state.\\n150: Each of these saves and retrieves state via a remote network service\\n151: appropriate to the backend that creates it.\\n152: \\n153: A state manager accepts and returns a state snapshot as a\\n154: [`states.State`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#State)\\n155: object. The state manager is responsible for exactly how that object is\\n156: serialized and stored, but all state managers at the time of writing use\\n157: the same JSON serialization format, storing the resulting JSON bytes in some\\n158: kind of arbitrary blob store.\\n159: \\n160: ## Graph Builder\\n161: \\n162: A _graph builder_ is called by a\\n163: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context)\\n164: method (e.g. `Plan` or `Apply`) to produce the graph that will be used\\n165: to represent the necessary steps for that operation and the dependency\\n166: relationships between them.\\n167: \\n168: In most cases, the\\n169: [vertices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_(graph_theory)) of Terraform's\\n170: graphs each represent a specific object in the configuration, or something\\n171: derived from those configuration objects. For example, each `resource` block\\n172: in the configuration has one corresponding\\n173: [`GraphNodeConfigResource`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeConfigResource)\\n174: vertex representing it in the \\\"plan\\\" graph. (Terraform Core uses terminology\\n175: inconsistently, describing graph _vertices_ also as graph _nodes_ in various\\n176: places. These both describe the same concept.)\\n177: \\n178: The [edges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_graph_theory_terms#edge)\\n179: in the graph represent \\\"must happen after\\\" relationships. These define the\\n180: order in which the vertices are evaluated, ensuring that e.g. one resource is\\n181: created before another resource that depends on it.\\n182: \\n183: Each operation has its own graph builder, because the graph building process\\n184: is different for each. For example, a \\\"plan\\\" operation needs a graph built\\n185: directly from the configuration, but an \\\"apply\\\" operation instead builds its\\n186: graph from the set of changes described in the plan that is being applied.\\n187: \\n188: The graph builders all work in terms of a sequence of _transforms_, which\\n189: are implementations of\\n190: [`terraform.GraphTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphTransformer).\\n191: Implementations of this interface just take a graph and mutate it in any\\n192: way needed, and so the set of available transforms is quite varied. Some\\n193: important examples include:\\n194: \\n195: * [`ConfigTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ConfigTransformer),\\n196:   which creates a graph vertex for each `resource` block in the configuration.\\n197: \\n198: * [`StateTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#StateTransformer),\\n199:   which creates a graph vertex for each resource instance currently tracked\\n200:   in the state.\\n201: \\n202: * [`ReferenceTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ReferenceTransformer),\\n203:   which analyses the configuration to find dependencies between resources and\\n204:   other objects and creates any necessary \\\"happens after\\\" edges for these.\\n205: \\n206: * [`ProviderTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ProviderTransformer),\\n207:   which associates each resource or resource instance with exactly one\\n208:   provider configuration (implementing\\n209:   [the inheritance rules](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/modules/develop/providers.html))\\n210:   and then creates \\\"happens after\\\" edges to ensure that the providers are\\n211:   initialized before taking any actions with the resources that belong to\\n212:   them.\\n213: \\n214: There are many more different graph transforms, which can be discovered\\n215: by reading the source code for the different graph builders. Each graph\\n216: builder uses a different subset of these depending on the needs of the\\n217: operation that is being performed.\\n218: \\n219: The result of graph building is a\\n220: [`terraform.Graph`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Graph), which\\n221: can then be processed using a _graph walker_.\\n222: \\n223: ## Graph Walk\\n224: \\n225: The process of walking the graph visits each vertex of that graph in a way\\n226: which respects the \\\"happens after\\\" edges in the graph. The walk algorithm\\n227: itself is implemented in\\n228: [the low-level `dag` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk)\\n229: (where \\\"DAG\\\" is short for [_Directed Acyclic Graph_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directed_acyclic_graph)), in\\n230: [`AcyclicGraph.Walk`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk).\\n231: However, the \\\"interesting\\\" Terraform walk functionality is implemented in\\n232: [`terraform.ContextGraphWalker`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ContextGraphWalker),\\n233: which implements a small set of higher-level operations that are performed\\n234: during the graph walk:\\n235: \\n236: * `EnterPath` is called once for each module in the configuration, taking a\\n237:   module address and returning a\\n238:   [`terraform.EvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#EvalContext)\\n239:   that tracks objects within that module. `terraform.Context` is the _global_\\n240:   context for the entire operation, while `terraform.EvalContext` is a\\n241:   context for processing within a single module, and is the primary means\\n242:   by which the namespaces in each module are kept separate.\\n243: \\n244: Each vertex in the graph is evaluated, in an order that guarantees that the\\n245: \\\"happens after\\\" edges will be respected. If possible, the graph walk algorithm\\n246: will evaluate multiple vertices concurrently. Vertex evaluation code must\\n247: therefore make careful use of concurrency primitives such as mutexes in order\\n248: to coordinate access to shared objects such as the `states.State` object.\\n249: In most cases, we use the helper wrapper\\n250: [`states.SyncState`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#SyncState)\\n251: to safely implement concurrent reads and writes from the shared state.\\n252: \\n253: ## Vertex Evaluation\\n254: \\n255: The action taken for each vertex during the graph walk is called\\n256: _execution_. Execution runs a sequence of arbitrary actions that make sense\\n257: for a particular vertex type.\\n258: \\n259: For example, evaluation of a vertex representing a resource instance during\\n260: a plan operation would include the following high-level steps:\\n261: \\n262: * Retrieve the resource's associated provider from the `EvalContext`. This\\n263:   should already be initialized earlier by the provider's own graph vertex,\\n264:   due to the \\\"happens after\\\" edge between the resource node and the provider\\n265:   node.\\n266: \\n267: * Retrieve from the state the portion relevant to the specific resource\\n268:   instance being evaluated.\\n269: \\n270: * Evaluate the attribute expressions given for the resource in configuration.\\n271:   This often involves retrieving the state of _other_ resource instances so\\n272:   that their values can be copied or transformed into the current instance's\\n273:   attributes, which is coordinated by the `EvalContext`.\\n274: \\n275: * Pass the current instance state and the resource configuration to the\\n276:   provider, asking the provider to produce an _instance diff_ representing the\\n277:   differences between the state and the configuration.\\n278: \\n279: * Save the instance diff as part of the plan that is being constructed by\\n280:   this operation.\\n281: \\n282: Each execution step for a vertex is an implementation of\\n283: [`terraform.Execute`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Execute).\\n284: As with graph transforms, the behavior of these implementations varies widely:\\n285: whereas graph transforms can take any action against the graph, an `Execute`\\n286: implementation can take any action against the `EvalContext`.\\n287: \\n288: The implementation of `terraform.EvalContext` used in real processing\\n289: (as opposed to testing) is\\n290: [`terraform.BuiltinEvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#BuiltinEvalContext).\\n291: It provides coordinated access to plugins, the current state, and the current\\n292: plan via the `EvalContext` interface methods.\\n293: \\n294: In order to be executed, a vertex must implement\\n295: [`terraform.GraphNodeExecutable`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeExecutable),\\n296: which has a single `Execute` method that handles. There are numerous `Execute`\\n297: implementations with different behaviors, but some prominent examples are:\\n298: \\n299: * [NodePlannableResource.Execute](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#NodePlannableResourceInstance.Execute), which handles the `plan` operation.\\n300: \\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/terraform--v1.9.0@96263511dba0a473b0636be30c58ee6c489e47ff/-/blob/docs/architecture.md%3FL1-300\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # Terraform Core Architecture Summary\n2: \n3: This document is a summary of the main components of Terraform Core and how\n4: data and requests flow between these components. It's intended as a primer\n5: to help navigate the codebase to dig into more details.\n6: \n7: We assume some familiarity with user-facing Terraform concepts like\n8: configuration, state, CLI workflow, etc. The Terraform website has\n9: documentation on these ideas.\n10: \n11: ## Terraform Request Flow\n12: \n13: The following diagram shows an approximation of how a user command is\n14: executed in Terraform:\n15: \n16: ![Terraform Architecture Diagram, described in text below](./images/architecture-overview.png)\n17: \n18: Each of the different subsystems (solid boxes) in this diagram is described\n19: in more detail in a corresponding section below.\n20: \n21: ## CLI (`command` package)\n22: \n23: Each time a user runs the `terraform` program, aside from some initial\n24: bootstrapping in the root package (not shown in the diagram) execution\n25: transfers immediately into one of the \"command\" implementations in\n26: [the `command` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/command).\n27: The mapping between the user-facing command names and\n28: their corresponding `command` package types can be found in the `commands.go`\n29: file in the root of the repository.\n30: \n31: The full flow illustrated above does not actually apply to _all_ commands,\n32: but it applies to the main Terraform workflow commands `terraform plan` and\n33: `terraform apply`, along with a few others.\n34: \n35: For these commands, the role of the command implementation is to read and parse\n36: any command line arguments, command line options, and environment variables\n37: that are needed for the given command and use them to produce a\n38: [`backend.Operation`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Operation)\n39: object that describes an action to be taken.\n40: \n41: An _operation_ consists of:\n42: \n43: * The action to be taken (e.g. \"plan\", \"apply\").\n44: * The name of the [workspace](https://www.terraform.io/docs/state/workspaces.html)\n45:   where the action will be taken.\n46: * Root module input variables to use for the action.\n47: * For the \"plan\" operation, a path to the directory containing the configuration's root module.\n48: * For the \"apply\" operation, the plan to apply.\n49: * Various other less-common options/settings such as `-target` addresses, the\n50: \"force\" flag, etc.\n51: \n52: The operation is then passed to the currently-selected\n53: [backend](https://www.terraform.io/docs/backends/index.html). Each backend name\n54: corresponds to an implementation of\n55: [`backend.Backend`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Backend), using a\n56: mapping table in\n57: [the `backend/init` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/init).\n58: \n59: Backends that are able to execute operations additionally implement\n60: [`backend.Enhanced`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Enhanced);\n61: the command-handling code calls `Operation` with the operation it has\n62: constructed, and then the backend is responsible for executing that action.\n63: \n64: Backends that execute operations, however, do so as an architectural implementation detail and not a\n65: general feature of backends. That is, the term 'backend' as a Terraform feature is used to refer to\n66: a plugin that determines where Terraform stores its state snapshots - only the default `local`\n67: backend and HCP Terraform's backends (`remote`, `cloud`) perform operations.\n68: \n69: Thus, most backends do _not_ implement this interface, and so the `command` package wraps these\n70: backends in an instance of\n71: [`local.Local`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/local#Local),\n72: causing the operation to be executed locally within the `terraform` process itself.\n73: \n74: ## Backends\n75: \n76: A _backend_ determines where Terraform should store its state snapshots.\n77: \n78: As described above, the `local` backend also executes operations on behalf of most other\n79: backends. It uses a _state manager_\n80: (either\n81: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) if the\n82: local backend is being used directly, or an implementation provided by whatever\n83: backend is being wrapped) to retrieve the current state for the workspace\n84: specified in the operation, then uses the _config loader_ to load and do\n85: initial processing/validation of the configuration specified in the\n86: operation. It then uses these, along with the other settings given in the\n87: operation, to construct a\n88: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context),\n89: which is the main object that actually performs Terraform operations.\n90: \n91: The `local` backend finally calls an appropriate method on that context to\n92: begin execution of the relevant command, such as\n93: [`Plan`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context.Plan)\n94: or\n95: [`Apply`](), which in turn constructs a graph using a _graph builder_,\n96: described in a later section.\n97: \n98: ## Configuration Loader\n99: \n100: The top-level configuration structure is represented by model types in\n101: [package `configs`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs).\n102: A whole configuration (the root module plus all of its descendent modules)\n103: is represented by\n104: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config).\n105: \n106: The `configs` package contains some low-level functionality for constructing\n107: configuration objects, but the main entry point is in the sub-package\n108: [`configload`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload]),\n109: via\n110: [`configload.Loader`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload#Loader).\n111: A loader deals with all of the details of installing child modules\n112: (during `terraform init`) and then locating those modules again when a\n113: configuration is loaded by a backend. It takes the path to a root module\n114: and recursively loads all of the child modules to produce a single\n115: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config)\n116: representing the entire configuration.\n117: \n118: Terraform expects configuration files written in the Terraform language, which\n119: is a DSL built on top of\n120: [HCL](https://github.com/hashicorp/hcl). Some parts of the configuration\n121: cannot be interpreted until we build and walk the graph, since they depend\n122: on the outcome of other parts of the configuration, and so these parts of\n123: the configuration remain represented as the low-level HCL types\n124: [`hcl.Body`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Body)\n125: and\n126: [`hcl.Expression`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Expression),\n127: allowing Terraform to interpret them at a more appropriate time.\n128: \n129: ## State Manager\n130: \n131: A _state manager_ is responsible for storing and retrieving snapshots of the\n132: [Terraform state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/index.html)\n133: for a particular workspace. Each manager is an implementation of\n134: some combination of interfaces in\n135: [the `statemgr` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr),\n136: with most practical managers implementing the full set of operations\n137: described by\n138: [`statemgr.Full`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Full)\n139: provided by a _backend_. The smaller interfaces exist primarily for use in\n140: other function signatures to be explicit about what actions the function might\n141: take on the state manager; there is little reason to write a state manager\n142: that does not implement all of `statemgr.Full`.\n143: \n144: The implementation\n145: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) is used\n146: by default (by the `local` backend) and is responsible for the familiar\n147: `terraform.tfstate` local file that most Terraform users start with, before\n148: they switch to [remote state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/remote.html).\n149: Other implementations of `statemgr.Full` are used to implement remote state.\n150: Each of these saves and retrieves state via a remote network service\n151: appropriate to the backend that creates it.\n152: \n153: A state manager accepts and returns a state snapshot as a\n154: [`states.State`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#State)\n155: object. The state manager is responsible for exactly how that object is\n156: serialized and stored, but all state managers at the time of writing use\n157: the same JSON serialization format, storing the resulting JSON bytes in some\n158: kind of arbitrary blob store.\n159: \n160: ## Graph Builder\n161: \n162: A _graph builder_ is called by a\n163: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context)\n164: method (e.g. `Plan` or `Apply`) to produce the graph that will be used\n165: to represent the necessary steps for that operation and the dependency\n166: relationships between them.\n167: \n168: In most cases, the\n169: [vertices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_(graph_theory)) of Terraform's\n170: graphs each represent a specific object in the configuration, or something\n171: derived from those configuration objects. For example, each `resource` block\n172: in the configuration has one corresponding\n173: [`GraphNodeConfigResource`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeConfigResource)\n174: vertex representing it in the \"plan\" graph. (Terraform Core uses terminology\n175: inconsistently, describing graph _vertices_ also as graph _nodes_ in various\n176: places. These both describe the same concept.)\n177: \n178: The [edges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_graph_theory_terms#edge)\n179: in the graph represent \"must happen after\" relationships. These define the\n180: order in which the vertices are evaluated, ensuring that e.g. one resource is\n181: created before another resource that depends on it.\n182: \n183: Each operation has its own graph builder, because the graph building process\n184: is different for each. For example, a \"plan\" operation needs a graph built\n185: directly from the configuration, but an \"apply\" operation instead builds its\n186: graph from the set of changes described in the plan that is being applied.\n187: \n188: The graph builders all work in terms of a sequence of _transforms_, which\n189: are implementations of\n190: [`terraform.GraphTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphTransformer).\n191: Implementations of this interface just take a graph and mutate it in any\n192: way needed, and so the set of available transforms is quite varied. Some\n193: important examples include:\n194: \n195: * [`ConfigTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ConfigTransformer),\n196:   which creates a graph vertex for each `resource` block in the configuration.\n197: \n198: * [`StateTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#StateTransformer),\n199:   which creates a graph vertex for each resource instance currently tracked\n200:   in the state.\n201: \n202: * [`ReferenceTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ReferenceTransformer),\n203:   which analyses the configuration to find dependencies between resources and\n204:   other objects and creates any necessary \"happens after\" edges for these.\n205: \n206: * [`ProviderTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ProviderTransformer),\n207:   which associates each resource or resource instance with exactly one\n208:   provider configuration (implementing\n209:   [the inheritance rules](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/modules/develop/providers.html))\n210:   and then creates \"happens after\" edges to ensure that the providers are\n211:   initialized before taking any actions with the resources that belong to\n212:   them.\n213: \n214: There are many more different graph transforms, which can be discovered\n215: by reading the source code for the different graph builders. Each graph\n216: builder uses a different subset of these depending on the needs of the\n217: operation that is being performed.\n218: \n219: The result of graph building is a\n220: [`terraform.Graph`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Graph), which\n221: can then be processed using a _graph walker_.\n222: \n223: ## Graph Walk\n224: \n225: The process of walking the graph visits each vertex of that graph in a way\n226: which respects the \"happens after\" edges in the graph. The walk algorithm\n227: itself is implemented in\n228: [the low-level `dag` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk)\n229: (where \"DAG\" is short for [_Directed Acyclic Graph_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directed_acyclic_graph)), in\n230: [`AcyclicGraph.Walk`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk).\n231: However, the \"interesting\" Terraform walk functionality is implemented in\n232: [`terraform.ContextGraphWalker`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ContextGraphWalker),\n233: which implements a small set of higher-level operations that are performed\n234: during the graph walk:\n235: \n236: * `EnterPath` is called once for each module in the configuration, taking a\n237:   module address and returning a\n238:   [`terraform.EvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#EvalContext)\n239:   that tracks objects within that module. `terraform.Context` is the _global_\n240:   context for the entire operation, while `terraform.EvalContext` is a\n241:   context for processing within a single module, and is the primary means\n242:   by which the namespaces in each module are kept separate.\n243: \n244: Each vertex in the graph is evaluated, in an order that guarantees that the\n245: \"happens after\" edges will be respected. If possible, the graph walk algorithm\n246: will evaluate multiple vertices concurrently. Vertex evaluation code must\n247: therefore make careful use of concurrency primitives such as mutexes in order\n248: to coordinate access to shared objects such as the `states.State` object.\n249: In most cases, we use the helper wrapper\n250: [`states.SyncState`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#SyncState)\n251: to safely implement concurrent reads and writes from the shared state.\n252: \n253: ## Vertex Evaluation\n254: \n255: The action taken for each vertex during the graph walk is called\n256: _execution_. Execution runs a sequence of arbitrary actions that make sense\n257: for a particular vertex type.\n258: \n259: For example, evaluation of a vertex representing a resource instance during\n260: a plan operation would include the following high-level steps:\n261: \n262: * Retrieve the resource's associated provider from the `EvalContext`. This\n263:   should already be initialized earlier by the provider's own graph vertex,\n264:   due to the \"happens after\" edge between the resource node and the provider\n265:   node.\n266: \n267: * Retrieve from the state the portion relevant to the specific resource\n268:   instance being evaluated.\n269: \n270: * Evaluate the attribute expressions given for the resource in configuration.\n271:   This often involves retrieving the state of _other_ resource instances so\n272:   that their values can be copied or transformed into the current instance's\n273:   attributes, which is coordinated by the `EvalContext`.\n274: \n275: * Pass the current instance state and the resource configuration to the\n276:   provider, asking the provider to produce an _instance diff_ representing the\n277:   differences between the state and the configuration.\n278: \n279: * Save the instance diff as part of the plan that is being constructed by\n280:   this operation.\n281: \n282: Each execution step for a vertex is an implementation of\n283: [`terraform.Execute`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Execute).\n284: As with graph transforms, the behavior of these implementations varies widely:\n285: whereas graph transforms can take any action against the graph, an `Execute`\n286: implementation can take any action against the `EvalContext`.\n287: \n288: The implementation of `terraform.EvalContext` used in real processing\n289: (as opposed to testing) is\n290: [`terraform.BuiltinEvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#BuiltinEvalContext).\n291: It provides coordinated access to plugins, the current state, and the current\n292: plan via the `EvalContext` interface methods.\n293: \n294: In order to be executed, a vertex must implement\n295: [`terraform.GraphNodeExecutable`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeExecutable),\n296: which has a single `Execute` method that handles. There are numerous `Execute`\n297: implementations with different behaviors, but some prominent examples are:\n298: \n299: * [NodePlannableResource.Execute](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#NodePlannableResourceInstance.Execute), which handles the `plan` operation.\n300: \n301: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/terraform--v1.9.0@96263511dba0a473b0636be30c58ee6c489e47ff/-/blob/docs/architecture.md%3FL1-300"

This function\n86: // can be called multiple times with each backend being \"live\" (usable)\n87: // one at a time.\n88: //\n89: // A side-effect of this method is the population of m.backendState, recording\n90: // the final resolved backend configuration after dealing with overrides from\n91: // the \"terraform init\" command line, etc.\n92: func (m *Meta) Backend(opts *BackendOpts) (backendrun.OperationsBackend, tfdiags.Diagnostics) {\n93: \tvar diags tfdiags.Diagnostics\n94: \n95: \t// If no opts are set, then initialize\n96: \tif opts == nil {\n97: \t\topts = &BackendOpts{}\n98: \t}\n99: \n100: \t// Initialize a backend from the config unless we're forcing a purely\n101: ",

  "content": "# Terraform State Backend Subsystem - Team Handoff Document\n\n## Overview\n\nThis document provides a comprehensive guide to the Terraform state backend subsystem, responsible for managing how Terraform stores, retrieves, and locks state files across different storage backends and operational modes. This system enables both local development and large-scale remote state management across teams.\n\n---\n\n## 1. Purpose\n\n### Problem Statement\n\nTerraform's state file is critical\u2014it maps the declared infrastructure in code to real-world resources. A robust backend system solves several key problems:\n\n1. **Shared State Management**: Multiple team members need safe concurrent access to the same state\n2. **State Persistence**: State must survive process restarts and failures\n3. **Storage Flexibility**: Different organizations need different storage solutions (local files, S3, Terraform Cloud, etc.)\n4. **State Integrity**: Concurrent writes must be atomic and prevent corruption\n5. **Workspace Isolation**: Teams need multiple independent state files (workspaces) within a single backend\n\n### Why Multiple Backend Types\n\nDifferent environments have different requirements:\n\n- **Local Backend** (`local`): Development, single-user, filesystem-based\n- **S3 Backend** (`s3`): Team collaboration, AWS-native, with optional DynamoDB locking\n- **Cloud/Remote Backend** (`cloud`/`remote`): Enterprise, full remote operations, HCP Terraform integration\n- **Other Backends** (`azurerm`, `gcs`, `consul`, `pg`, `kubernetes`, etc.): Cloud-provider-specific and specialized solutions\n\n### Key Responsibilities of a Backend\n\n1. **State Manager Provisioning**: Provide a `statemgr.Full` implementation for state storage\n2. **Configuration Management**: Define and validate backend configuration via schema\n3. **Workspace Support**: Handle multiple named workspaces\n4. **Optional Operation Execution**: Some backends (local, remote, cloud) can execute Terraform operations directly\n5. **State Locking** (optional): Prevent concurrent modifications via mutual exclusion\n6. **Metadata Preservation**: Track state serial/lineage for consistency verification\n\n---\n\n## 2. Dependencies\n\n### Upstream Dependencies (What Calls Into Backends)\n\n```\nCommand Layer\n    \u2193\nmeta_backend.go (Backend initialization)\n    \u2193\nBackend Interface (Configuration & State Management)\n```\n\n**Key Entry Points:**\n- `internal/command/meta_backend.go:Backend()` - CLI initializes backends and performs operations\n- `internal/command/init.go` - Backend initialization and reconfiguration\n- `internal/command/output.go` - Reads outputs from state via backend\n- Various operation commands (`plan.go`, `apply.go`, etc.)\n\n### Internal Dependencies (What Backends Use)\n\n1. **State Manager Interfaces** (`internal/states/statemgr/`):\n   - `statemgr.Full`: Union of Transient, Persistent, and Locker interfaces\n   - `statemgr.Locker`: State locking via Lock/Unlock\n   - `statemgr.Persistent`: RefreshState/PersistState for durable storage\n   - `statemgr.Transient`: Reader/Writer for in-memory state\n\n2. **State Objects** (`internal/states/`):\n   - `states.State`: The actual state data structure\n   - `statefile.File`: Serialized state with metadata (serial, lineage, version)\n\n3. **Configuration System**:\n   - `internal/configs/configschema`: Schema definition for backend configuration\n   - `cty.Value`: Configuration values after parsing\n   - `tfdiags.Diagnostics`: Error and warning reporting\n\n4. **Local Backend** (`internal/backend/local/`):\n   - Wraps remote/simple backends and adds operation execution\n   - Manages state locking, refreshing, and persistence\n   - Integrates with `terraform.Context` for plan/apply operations\n\n5. **Backend Initialization** (`internal/backend/init/`):\n   - Global registry of all available backends\n   - Backend lookup and instantiation\n\n### Downstream Dependencies (What Backends Call)\n\n1. **Storage Providers**:\n   - S3: AWS SDK v2 (`aws-sdk-go-v2`)\n   - GCS: Google Cloud Client Library\n   - Azure: Azure SDK for Go\n   - PostgreSQL: `pq` driver\n   - Consul: Consul HTTP API\n   - Kubernetes: Kubernetes client library\n\n2. **Operation Execution** (for OperationsBackend implementations):\n   - `terraform.Context` and graph builders for plan/apply\n   - Provider system for resource operations\n   - Module loader for configuration loading\n\n### Integration with Broader Architecture\n\n```\n\u250c\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2510\n\u2502                        CLI Commands                          \u2502\n\u2502                   (plan, apply, init, etc)                  \u2502\n\u2514\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u252c\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2518\n                     \u2502\n                     \u2193\n      \u250c\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2510\n                \u2193              \u2193\n          State Manager  Configuration\n          (statemgr)     Schema\n                \u2502              \u2502\n                   \u2193\n    Storage Providers       State Objects\n    (S3, GCS, etc)         & Serialization\n```\n\n---\n\n## 3. Relevant Components\n\n### Main Backend Interface\n\n**File**: `internal/backend/backend.go`\n\n**Core Interface**:\n```go\ntype Backend interface {\n    // Schema and configuration\n    ConfigSchema() *configschema.Block\n    PrepareConfig(cty.Value) (cty.Value, tfdiags.Diagnostics)\n    Configure(cty.Value) tfdiags.Diagnostics\n\n    // State management\n    StateMgr(workspace string) (statemgr.Full, error)\n    DeleteWorkspace(name string, force bool) error\n    Workspaces() ([]string, error)\n}\n```\n\n**Key Constants**:\n- `DefaultStateName = \"default\"` - Every backend must have this workspace\n- `ErrWorkspacesNotSupported` - Returned by single-workspace backends\n- `ErrDefaultWorkspaceNotSupported` - Some backends don't support default\n\n### State Manager Interfaces\n\n**File**: `internal/states/statemgr/`\n\n**Core Hierarchy**:\n```\nFull (union of all below)\n  \u251c\u2500\u2500 Storage\n  \u2502   \u251c\u2500\u2500 Transient (Reader + Writer)\n  \u2502   \u2514\u2500\u2500 Persistent (Refresher + Persister + OutputReader)\n  \u2514\u2500\u2500 Locker (Lock + Unlock for mutual exclusion)\n```\n\n**Transient Interface** (`transient.go`):\n- `State() *states.State` - Get current state snapshot\n- `WriteState(*states.State) error` - Update in-memory state\n\n**Persistent Interface** (`persistent.go`):\n- `RefreshState() error` - Load state from durable storage\n- `PersistState(*schemarepo.Schemas) error` - Save state to durable storage\n- `GetRootOutputValues(ctx) (map[string]*states.OutputValue, error)` - Output-only reads\n\n**Locker Interface** (`locker.go`):\n- `Lock(info *LockInfo) (string, error)` - Acquire mutual exclusion lock\n- `Unlock(id string) error` - Release lock\n- `LockWithContext(ctx, Locker, LockInfo) (string, error)` - Helper with retry logic\n\n**Optional Interfaces**:\n- `Migrator` - Preserves serial/lineage during state migrations\n- `PersistentMeta` - Query snapshot metadata (serial, lineage, version)\n- `IntermediateStateConditionalPersister` - Control intermediate state snapshot rules\n\n### Backend Initialization System\n\n**File**: `internal/backend/init/init.go`\n\n**Global Registry**:\n```go\nvar backends map[string]backend.InitFn  // Global backend registry\n\n// Available backends (as of v1.9.0):\n// \"local\" -> local.New()\n// \"s3\" -> s3.New()\n// \"azurerm\" -> azure.New()\n// \"gcs\" -> gcs.New()\n// \"consul\" -> consul.New()\n// \"http\" -> http.New()\n// \"inmem\" -> inmem.New()  // Testing only\n// \"kubernetes\" -> kubernetes.New()\n// \"oss\" -> oss.New()\n// \"pg\" -> pg.New()\n// \"remote\" -> remote.New()\n// \"cloud\" -> cloud.New()  // HCP Terraform\n```\n\n**Functions**:\n- `Init(disco *Disco)` - Initialize the registry (called at startup)\n- `Backend(name string) backend.InitFn` - Lookup a backend factory\n- `Set(name string, f backend.InitFn)` - Register a custom backend\n\n### Local Backend\n\n**Files**: `internal/backend/local/`\n- `backend.go` - Core Local type and state management\n- `backend_local.go` - Enhanced operations (plan, apply, refresh)\n- `backend_plan.go` - Plan execution\n- `backend_apply.go` - Apply execution\n- `backend_refresh.go` - Refresh execution\n- `cli.go` - CLI integration and prompts\n- `hook_state.go` - State update hooks for intermediate snapshots\n\n**Key Type**:\n```go\ntype Local struct {\n    StatePath string                    // Path to state file\n    StateOutPath string                 // Output path (defaults to StatePath)\n    StateBackupPath string              // Backup location\n    StateWorkspaceDir string            // Directory for named workspaces\n\n    Backend backend.Backend             // Wrapped remote backend (if any)\n    ContextOpts *terraform.ContextOpts  // Context configuration\n    states map[string]statemgr.Full    // Cached state managers\n}\n```\n\n**Implements**:\n- `backend.Backend` (basic state management)\n- `backendrun.OperationsBackend` (can execute plan/apply/refresh)\n- `backendrun.Local` (local execution interface)\n\n### Remote State Backends\n\n**Files**: `internal/backend/remote-state/`\n\nEach backend has a similar structure:\n- `backend.go` - Backend implementation\n- `client.go` - Storage client (S3, GCS, etc.)\n- `backend_test.go` - Acceptance tests\n\n**Main Implementations**:\n\n1. **S3** (`s3/`): Most complex, provides full locking via DynamoDB\n2. **GCS** (`gcs/`): Google Cloud Storage\n3. **Azure** (`azure/`): Azure Blob Storage\n4. **Consul** (`consul/`): Distributed key-value store\n5. **PostgreSQL** (`pg/`): SQL-based state storage\n6. **Kubernetes** (`kubernetes/`): K8s native state storage\n7. **HTTP** (`http/`): Generic HTTP backend\n8. **OSS** (`oss/`): Alibaba Object Storage Service\n9. **COS** (`cos/`): Tencent Cloud Object Storage\n\n### Cloud/Remote Backend\n\n**Files**: `internal/cloud/`\n- `backend.go` - Full HCP Terraform integration\n- `state.go` - State manager with Terraform Cloud API\n- `testing.go` - Test utilities\n\n**Special Features**:\n- Executes operations on HCP Terraform's servers\n- Handles workspace-to-run mapping\n- Version conflict detection\n- Cost estimation and policy enforcement\n\n### Backend Configuration Loading\n\n**File**: `internal/command/meta_backend.go`\n\n**Key Functions**:\n- `Backend(opts *BackendOpts) (backendrun.OperationsBackend, tfdiags.Diagnostics)` - Load and initialize\n- `BackendConfig()` - Get current backend configuration\n- `backendMigrateState()` - Migrate state between backends\n- `backendConfigNeedsMigration()` - Detect backend changes\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes\n\n### Configuration Errors\n\n**Detection**: `PrepareConfig()` and `Configure()` methods\n\nCommon issues:\n- Missing required config values (bucket name, table name, etc.)\n- Invalid credential formats\n- Invalid region specifications\n- Deprecated backend configurations\n\n**Handling**: Returned as `tfdiags.Diagnostics` with contextual information\n\n**Example S3 Validation** (`s3/backend.go:PrepareConfig()`):\n```go\n// Checks bucket existence, DynamoDB table, KMS key access, account IDs\n// Returns clear diagnostics for missing/misconfigured resources\n```\n\n### State Locking Failures\n\n**Lock Contention**: When state is already locked\n\n**Mechanism** (`locker.go:LockWithContext()`):\n```go\n// Retry logic with exponential backoff\n// Max delay: 16 seconds\n// Returns LockError with existing lock info\n// Allows force-unlock via CLI\n```\n\n**Timeout Handling**:\n- Context cancellation stops retry attempts\n- Returns last LockError to user\n- User can use `terraform force-unlock <ID>` to break stale locks\n\n**Common Issues**:\n- Process crash with held lock (state remains locked)\n- Stale locks from failed CI/CD runs\n- Network partitions preventing lock release\n\n### Storage Unavailability\n\n**S3 Examples**:\n- Bucket doesn't exist \u2192 RefreshState/PersistState returns error\n- No AWS credentials \u2192 Configure fails with auth error\n- Network timeout \u2192 Persistence operations timeout\n- S3 access denied \u2192 RefreshState/PersistState fails with 403\n\n**Handling**:\n- Operations fail with clear error messages\n- Terraform aborts before state corruption\n- User must resolve underlying issue (credentials, network, permissions)\n\n### State Consistency Issues\n\n**Serial/Lineage Mismatch** (`.stateSnapshotMeta()`):\n- Serial is incremented with each successful persist\n- Lineage tracks state file generations\n- Mismatches indicate concurrent modifications or rollback attempts\n\n**Detection**: In S3 backend's `PersistState()` via DynamoDB conditionally checked against expected serial/lineage\n\n**Corruption Scenarios**:\n1. Direct state file modification (bypassing backends)\n2. Multiple concurrent Terraform runs without locking\n3. Forced state overwrites without lineage/serial preservation\n\n**Prevention**:\n- Always use backends with locking support for team environments\n- `terraform push`/`pull` preserve metadata via `Migrator` interface\n- Versioning in remote storage (S3 versioning, Git, etc.)\n\n### Configuration Change Detection\n\n**File**: `meta_backend.go:BackendConfig()` and `backendConfigNeedsMigration()`\n\nWhen backend type or config changes, Terraform detects and prompts user to:\n1. Review differences\n2. Choose to migrate state between backends\n3. Confirm force migration if serial/lineage checks fail\n\n### Workspace-Specific Failures\n\n- `DeleteWorkspace(\"default\", ...)` always fails - default is mandatory\n- Creating workspaces on single-workspace backends returns `ErrWorkspacesNotSupported`\n- Workspace directory corruption prevents new workspace creation\n\n---\n\n## 5. Testing\n\n### Test Patterns\n\n**File**: `internal/backend/testing.go`\n\nCore test helpers:\n\n1. **`TestBackendConfig(t, backend, hclBody)`**\n   - Validates schema, prepares config, configures backend\n   - Common starting point for all backend tests\n   - Handles diagnostics and fails on errors\n\n2. **`TestBackendStates(t, backend)`**\n   - Creates multiple workspaces (foo, bar, default)\n   - Writes distinct states to each\n   - Verifies isolation and persistence\n   - Tests workspace creation/deletion/listing\n   - Ensures default workspace cannot be deleted\n\n3. **`TestBackendStateLocks(t, b1, b2)`**\n   - Creates two backend instances\n   - Tests mutual exclusion (b1 locks, b2 cannot lock)\n   - Verifies lock metadata (Who, Operation, Created)\n   - Tests lock persistence and timeout\n\n4. **`TestBackendStateForceUnlock(t, b1, b2)`**\n   - Tests `-force-unlock` capability\n   - Requires lock ID extraction from error\n\n### Test Structure for New Backends\n\n**File Pattern**: `internal/backend/remote-state/<BACKEND>/<backend>_test.go`\n\n**Example: S3 Backend** (`s3/backend_test.go`):\n```go\n// Setup\nfunc TestBackend(t *testing.T) {\n    t.Parallel()\n    testACC(t)  // Skip if not in acceptance test mode\n\n    // Get AWS credentials and S3 bucket\n    region := os.Getenv(\"AWS_DEFAULT_REGION\")\n    bucketName := \"terraform-backends-tests-\" + randomSuffix\n\n    // Create two backend instances\n    b1 := backend.TestBackendConfig(t, New(), backend.TestWrapConfig(map[string]interface{}{\n        \"bucket\": bucketName,\n        \"key\": \"state\",\n        \"region\": region,\n        \"dynamodb_table\": tableName,  // For locking\n    }))\n\n    b2 := backend.TestBackendConfig(t, New(), backend.TestWrapConfig(map[string]interface{}{...}))\n\n    // Run standard tests\n    backend.TestBackendStates(t, b1)\n    backend.TestBackendStateLocks(t, b1, b2)\n}\n```\n\n### Acceptance Test Setup\n\nBackends that interact with external systems require acceptance tests:\n- S3: Real AWS credentials\n- GCS: Real GCP credentials\n- Azure: Real Azure credentials\n- Consul: Running Consul server\n- PostgreSQL: Running Postgres server\n\n**Gating**: Tests skip if `TF_ACC` environment variable not set\n\n### State Manager Testing\n\n**File**: `statemgr_fake.go` provides fake implementations:\n- `fakeErrorFull` - Fails all operations\n- Used for testing error handling in higher-level code\n\n---\n\n## 6. Debugging\n\n### Logging\n\n**Environment Variables**:\n- `TF_LOG=DEBUG` - Enable debug logging\n- `TF_LOG_PATH=/path/to/log` - Write to file\n- `TF_LOG=TRACE` - Most verbose logging\n\n**Log Output Locations**:\n- Local backend: `internal/backend/local/` operations logged\n- S3 backend: AWS SDK v2 logging, endpoint calls\n- Locking: `statemgr.Locker` Lock/Unlock calls logged\n\n**Key Log Messages**:\n```\n// Backend selection\n\"...Backend configuration changed!...\"\n\n// Lock operations\n\"TestBackend: testing state locking for %T\"\n\n// State refresh/persist\n\"refreshing state...\"\n\"persisting state...\"\n```\n\n### Verification Tools\n\n**1. State Inspection** (`terraform state list/show`):\n```bash\nterraform state list                     # List all resources\nterraform state show resource.id         # Show specific resource\nterraform state pull > state.json        # Export state locally\n```\n\n**2. Lock Status Checking**:\n```bash\nterraform state show -raw output       # See lock metadata in outputs\n# Or inspect storage directly (S3, DynamoDB, Consul, etc.)\n```\n\n**3. Remote State Diagnosis**:\n```bash\n# For S3 backend\naws s3api head-object --bucket BUCKET --key KEY  # Check state file\naws dynamodb scan --table-name TABLE              # Check locks\n\n# For Consul\nconsul kv get terraform/state/default\n\n# For PostgreSQL\nSELECT * FROM states WHERE name = 'default';\n```\n\n**4. Configuration Inspection**:\n```bash\nterraform init -backend=false           # Skip backend init\nterraform backends                      # List configured backends\ncat .terraform/terraform.tfstate         # Backend state metadata\n```\n\n### Common Issues and Resolution\n\n**Issue: State is locked by another process**\n\nDiagnosis:\n1. Check lock metadata: Extract lock ID from error message\n2. Verify process is not actually running: `ps aux | grep terraform`\n3. Check storage lock entry (DynamoDB, Consul, file system)\n\nResolution:\n```bash\nterraform force-unlock <LOCK_ID>\n# or manually delete lock from storage if urgent\n```\n\n**Issue: State file corruption or serial mismatch**\n\nDiagnosis:\n```bash\nterraform state pull | jq '.version, .serial, .lineage'\n# Compare with remote storage\n```\n\nResolution:\n```bash\n# Manual state push (preserves serial/lineage via Migrator)\nterraform state push -force backup.tfstate\n\n# Or export/import via pull/push\nterraform state pull > backup.json\n# ... make manual fixes ...\nterraform state push backup.json\n```\n\n**Issue: Backend configuration conflicts**\n\nDiagnosis:\n```bash\n# When backend config changes\nterraform init  # Shows migration prompts\n```\n\nResolution:\n```bash\nterraform init -migrate-state   # Automatic migration\n# or\nterraform init -reconfigure     # Abandon old backend\n```\n\n**Issue: S3 bucket access denied**\n\nDiagnosis:\n```bash\naws s3api head-object --bucket BUCKET --key KEY\n# or\naws s3api get-bucket-versioning --bucket BUCKET\n```\n\nResolution:\n```bash\n# Verify IAM policy includes:\n# s3:GetObject, s3:PutObject, s3:DeleteObject\n# s3:GetBucketVersioning (if using versioning)\n# dynamodb:DescribeTable, dynamodb:PutItem, etc. (if using DynamoDB locks)\n```\n\n**Issue: Stale lock not releasing**\n\nDiagnosis:\n```bash\n# For S3 + DynamoDB locking\naws dynamodb get-item --table-name LOCKS --key '{\"LockID\":{\"S\":\"path/to/state\"}}'\n\n# Extract lock info\nterraform state show terraform_remote_state  # Won't work if locked\n```\n\nResolution:\n```bash\n# Delete stale lock\nterraform force-unlock <LOCK_ID>\n\n# Or directly from DynamoDB\naws dynamodb delete-item --table-name LOCKS \\\n  --key '{\"LockID\":{\"S\":\"path/to/state\"}}'\n```\n\n---\n\n## 7. Adding a New Backend\n\n### Overview\n\nAdding a new backend requires implementing the `backend.Backend` interface and registering it in the initialization system. For remote-only backends (no operations), this is straightforward. For operation-executing backends, implementation is more complex.\n\n### Step-by-Step Process\n\n#### 1. Create Backend Package Structure\n\n```\ninternal/backend/remote-state/<BACKEND>/\n  \u251c\u2500\u2500 backend.go           # Core Backend type and methods\n  \u251c\u2500\u2500 client.go            # Storage client implementation\n  \u251c\u2500\u2500 backend_test.go      # Acceptance tests\n  \u2514\u2500\u2500 testdata/            # Test fixtures if needed\n```\n\n#### 2. Implement Backend Type\n\n**File**: `backend.go`\n\n```go\npackage mybackend\n\nimport (\n    \"github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend\"\n    \"github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configschema\"\n    \"github.com/zclconf/go-cty/cty\"\n    \"github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr\"\n    \"github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/tfdiags\"\n)\n\ntype Backend struct {\n    // Storage configuration\n    clientConfig *ClientConfig\n    client       *Client\n\n    // State managers cache\n    states map[string]statemgr.Full\n}\n\n// ConfigSchema returns the schema for backend configuration\nfunc (b *Backend) ConfigSchema() *configschema.Block {\n    return &configschema.Block{\n        Attributes: map[string]*configschema.Attribute{\n            \"bucket\": {\n                Type:        cty.String,\n                Required:    true,\n                Description: \"The storage bucket\",\n            },\n            \"region\": {\n                Type:        cty.String,\n                Optional:    true,\n                Description: \"Storage region\",\n            },\n            // ... more config options\n        },\n    }\n}\n\n// PrepareConfig validates and prepares configuration\nfunc (b *Backend) PrepareConfig(obj cty.Value) (cty.Value, tfdiags.Diagnostics) {\n    var diags tfdiags.Diagnostics\n\n    // Validate required fields are present\n    if obj.IsNull() {\n        diags = diags.Append(tfdiags.SimpleError(\"Backend configuration is required\"))\n        return obj, diags\n    }\n\n    // Insert defaults if needed\n    // Return prepared config\n\n    return obj, diags\n}\n\n// Configure initializes the backend with configuration\nfunc (b *Backend) Configure(obj cty.Value) tfdiags.Diagnostics {\n    var diags tfdiags.Diagnostics\n\n    // Extract configuration values\n    config := &ClientConfig{}\n\n    val := obj.GetAttr(\"bucket\")\n    if !val.IsNull() {\n        config.Bucket = val.AsString()\n    }\n\n    // Validate external resources (bucket exists, credentials valid, etc.)\n    client, err := NewClient(config)\n    if err != nil {\n        diags = diags.Append(tfdiags.SimpleError(fmt.Sprintf(\n            \"Failed to configure backend: %s\", err)))\n        return diags\n    }\n\n    b.client = client\n    b.states = make(map[string]statemgr.Full)\n\n    return diags\n}\n\n// StateMgr returns a state manager for the given workspace\nfunc (b *Backend) StateMgr(workspace string) (statemgr.Full, error) {\n    // Return cached state manager if exists\n    if s, ok := b.states[workspace]; ok {\n        return s, nil\n    }\n\n    // Create new state manager\n    s := &State{\n        client:    b.client,\n        workspace: workspace,\n    }\n\n    b.states[workspace] = s\n    return s, nil\n}\n\n// Workspaces returns all available workspaces\nfunc (b *Backend) Workspaces() ([]string, error) {\n    // List all states in storage\n    return b.client.ListStates()\n}\n\n// DeleteWorkspace deletes a workspace\nfunc (b *Backend) DeleteWorkspace(name string, force bool) error {\n    if name == backend.DefaultStateName {\n        return fmt.Errorf(\"cannot delete default workspace\")\n    }\n\n    delete(b.states, name)\n    return b.client.DeleteState(name)\n}\n\n// New is the factory function\nfunc New() backend.Backend {\n    return &Backend{}\n}\n```\n\n#### 3. Implement State Manager\n\n**File**: `client.go` or `state.go`\n\n```go\ntype State struct {\n    client    *Client\n    workspace string\n\n    // In-memory state\n    state *states.State\n\n    // Metadata for locking (optional)\n    lockID string\n}\n\n// Transient interface - Reader/Writer\nfunc (s *State) State() *states.State {\n    return s.state\n}\n\nfunc (s *State) WriteState(st *states.State) error {\n    s.state = st\n    return nil\n}\n\n// Persistent interface - Refresher/Persister\nfunc (s *State) RefreshState() error {\n    data, err := s.client.GetState(s.workspace)\n    if err != nil {\n        return err\n    }\n\n    // Deserialize JSON to states.State\n    s.state = parseState(data)\n    return nil\n}\n\nfunc (s *State) PersistState(schemas *schemarepo.Schemas) error {\n    // Serialize state to JSON\n    data := serializeState(s.state)\n\n    // Write to storage with atomicity check\n    return s.client.PutState(s.workspace, data, expectedSerial)\n}\n\nfunc (s *State) GetRootOutputValues(ctx context.Context) (map[string]*states.OutputValue, error) {\n    if s.state == nil {\n        return nil, nil\n    }\n    return s.state.RootModule().OutputValues, nil\n}\n\n// Optional Locker interface for state locking\nfunc (s *State) Lock(info *statemgr.LockInfo) (string, error) {\n    lockErr := s.client.AcquireLock(s.workspace, info)\n    if lockErr != nil {\n        return \"\", &statemgr.LockError{\n            Info: info,\n            Err:  lockErr,\n        }\n    }\n\n    s.lockID = info.ID\n    return info.ID, nil\n}\n\nfunc (s *State) Unlock(id string) error {\n    return s.client.ReleaseLock(s.workspace, id)\n}\n\n// Verify implementation\nvar _ statemgr.Full = (*State)(nil)\n```\n\n#### 4. Implement Storage Client\n\n**File**: `client.go`\n\n```go\ntype Client struct {\n    config *ClientConfig\n    // SDK clients (AWS SDK, Google Cloud SDK, etc.)\n}\n\ntype ClientConfig struct {\n    Bucket string\n    Region string\n    // ... other config\n}\n\nfunc NewClient(config *ClientConfig) (*Client, error) {\n    // Initialize SDK clients\n    // Validate access (test credentials, list buckets, etc.)\n    return &Client{config: config}, nil\n}\n\nfunc (c *Client) GetState(workspace string) ([]byte, error) {\n    // Fetch state file from storage\n    // Handle not-found gracefully\n}\n\nfunc (c *Client) PutState(workspace string, data []byte, serial uint64) error {\n    // Write state with serial/lineage consistency check\n    // Use atomicity features of storage backend\n}\n\nfunc (c *Client) DeleteState(workspace string) error {\n    // Delete state file for workspace\n}\n\nfunc (c *Client) ListStates() ([]string, error) {\n    // List all state files, return workspace names\n}\n\nfunc (c *Client) AcquireLock(workspace string, info *statemgr.LockInfo) error {\n    // Implement mutual exclusion\n    // Return error if already locked\n}\n\nfunc (c *Client) ReleaseLock(workspace string, id string) error {\n    // Release lock\n}\n```\n\n#### 5. Write Acceptance Tests\n\n**File**: `backend_test.go`\n\n```go\npackage mybackend\n\nimport (\n    \"os\"\n    \"testing\"\n\n    \"github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend\"\n)\n\nfunc testACC(t *testing.T) {\n    if os.Getenv(\"TF_ACC\") == \"\" {\n        t.Skip(\"skipping acceptance test\")\n    }\n}\n\nfunc TestBackend_impl(t *testing.T) {\n    var _ backend.Backend = new(Backend)\n}\n\nfunc TestBackend(t *testing.T) {\n    testACC(t)\n\n    // Setup storage\n    bucket := \"tf-test-\" + randomString()\n\n    // Create backend\n    b := backend.TestBackendConfig(t, New(), backend.TestWrapConfig(map[string]interface{}{\n        \"bucket\": bucket,\n    }))\n\n    // Run standard tests\n    backend.TestBackendStates(t, b)\n}\n\nfunc TestBackendStateLocks(t *testing.T) {\n    testACC(t)\n\n    bucket := \"tf-test-\" + randomString()\n\n    b1 := backend.TestBackendConfig(t, New(), backend.TestWrapConfig(map[string]interface{}{\n        \"bucket\": bucket,\n    }))\n\n    b2 := backend.TestBackendConfig(t, New(), backend.TestWrapConfig(map[string]interface{}{\n        \"bucket\": bucket,\n    }))\n\n    backend.TestBackendStateLocks(t, b1, b2)\n}\n```\n\n#### 6. (Optional) Implement Migration Support\n\nIf you want to preserve state serial/lineage during migrations:\n\n```go\n// Implement optional Migrator interface\nfunc (s *State) StateForMigration() *statefile.File {\n    return &statefile.File{\n        State: s.state,\n        Meta: statefile.Meta{\n            Version:   statefile.Version,\n            Lineage:   s.lineage,\n            Serial:    s.serial,\n            TfVersion: version.Version,\n        },\n    }\n}\n\nfunc (s *State) WriteStateForMigration(f *statefile.File, force bool) error {\n    s.state = f.State\n    s.lineage = f.Meta.Lineage\n    s.serial = f.Meta.Serial\n\n    // Validate if not forced\n    if !force {\n        // Check existing serial/lineage matches...\n    }\n\n    return s.PersistState(nil)\n}\n```\n\n#### 8.
